actually means let the buyer beware because he should not be ignorant of the nature of the property he is buying so as a legal Doctrine this means the burden is placed on the purchaser to make a reasonable good faith effort to examine the property they're purchasing so if they don't look at the property closely and they buy it they then find out there's something wrong with it that's too bad of objects is really important in the museum world so I want to give you an example of how things can go very wrong when you're acquiring and accessioning objects in a collection if you don't do good that the areas has extraordinary archaeological and cultural record and that it was profoundly sacred to many Native American tribes eleven months later the next president which was Donald Trump - Law 101: How a Lawsuit Works: Kind: captions Language: en - Thanks to Dashlane for keeping Legal Eagle in the air. Have you ever wanted to know how a lawsuit works? Most people think of lawsuits as being these big scary animals that are completely opaque and esoteric. But the truth is, they are vital to the functioning of our society and can right wrongs where they occur. But today I wanna demystify the process so that if you ever need to use a lawsuit or find yourself in the middle of one, you'll at least know how they operate. Hey Legal Eagles, it's time to think like a lawyer. Today we are covering civil lawsuits or as most people refer to them, just lawsuits. As always, be sure to subscribe and comment in the form of an objection, which I will either sustain or overrule. I wanted to cover lawsuits today because there are a lot of misconceptions about how the civil process works. And let me start out this discussion by drawing a distinction between the civil process of law and the criminal process. The criminal process is probably the one that you see most often on TV and in movies. That is where you have committed a crime and you are prosecuted by the state. And generally the penalties for a criminal offense is jail time. Sometimes there are monetary penalties that go along with a crime but the bottom line is that generally speaking the criminal process involves the state prosecuting you for crimes against society. The civil process on the other hand is different. The civil process is how you get compensation for damages that are caused by another person. Damages that are caused in the form of usually a breach of contract or what we call torts, which are civil wrongs, things that are not crimes but are violations of norms such that we believe that one person should compensate the other. Generally speaking, the end goal of the civil process is to receive monetary compensation for the wrongs that you have suffered. As opposed to the criminal process where you have done a wrong against society and you can go to jail as a result. So today we're going to be talking exclusively about the civil law process, not the criminal process and the main tool of the civil process which is of course is the lawsuit. Now of course, there are always a million exceptions to the things I'm going to talk about which is why there are lawyers and you should always consult a lawyer before engaging in any kind of civil process. But today I wanna go over the broad strokes so you have a general idea of how a lawsuit works. A lawsuit starts with what's called a complaint. A complaint is the document that initiates your civil suit against somebody else. It require a couple of things. Primarily it is your factual statement of allegations as to what happened. How you have been wronged and why you are entitled to some sort of compensation. It contains a statement of proper jurisdiction and venue to show that this particular court is the one that should be hearing this particular lawsuit. But for the most part it contains your factual allegations. Your story of what happened. And at the end of the complaint there Is this statement of law that explains the causes of action that give rise to your particular claim. So after you have the factual statement about what happened, you'll have the legal statement about why those particular facts fit the elements of a particular cause of action or claim and that entitles you to So for example, a few weeks back I covered the Fornite dance controversy. Where certain artists were suing video game manufacturers, claiming that they had stolen their copyrighted dance. In their complaints, they laid out that these particular artists had in fact created a copyrightable works in the form of dances. So that was the factual predicate. They laid out that the video game manufacturers had taken these copyrighted works, used them without permission, again the factual predicate, and then at the end they stated that this was a violation of copyright law, citing to the federal standard for copyright infringement. So that's an example what the complaint looks like, it's the initiating document, it contains the factual and legal reasoning for why you think you should get compensation from another person. The next step after someone files a complaint, generally speaking, is to file what's called an answer or a motion to dismiss. The person who files the complaint is called the plaintiff. And generally speaking, the person who responds to the complaint is the defendant. Now once the plaintiff files the complaint, the defendant has basically two options. One is called an answer, the other is called a motion to dismiss. The answer, not surprisingly, is an answering document to the complaint. Which goes line by line over the factual allegations in the complaint and either admits to those particular facts or denies them. Now it might seem strange to admit anything in a complaint but really in any given lawsuit there will be things that both sides agree to, facts that are not really in dispute and facts that they deny and that they say never happened. So the purpose of the answer is to stipulate the facts that are not in dispute and to deny the facts that are in dispute. The second option, if it is available, is called a motion to dismiss. A motion to dismiss doesn't challenge the facts of the complaint, there will be time for that later in the lawsuit. The motion to dismiss simply says that even if everything that the plaintiff says is 100% true from a factual standpoint, it still doesn't give rise to any kind of legal remedy. It is legally invalid. So let's say that someone files a lawsuit against you for being a big mean jerk face. Well there's no cause of action called jerk face. The law doesn't allow you to get compensation just because someone was a jerk face to you. That might make you a bad person but that doesn't mean that you have an obligation to pay them any sort of money. However, let's say that someone files a claim for defamation. Defamation requires one, a false statement of a fact rather than an opinion, publication or communication of that statement, and fault amounting to at least negligence and then suffering damages as a result of that untrue fact statement. Now let's assume that the complaint alleging defamation is 100% factually incorrect. You either never said the statement in question or the statements are true and therefor couldn't have been a false fact that was communicated. In that circumstance a motion to dismiss wouldn't work. Because a motion to dismiss assumes that the facts in the complaint are true. A motion to dismiss only tests the legal sufficiency of the complaint. And in this particular case, someone has alleged legal predicate for defamation. There will be time later on to show that the facts are wrong but effectively at these early stages the only things that you can do as a defendant are either answer or file a motion to dismiss. Which only tests the legal sufficiency of the complaint not the factual sufficiency. And if you lose a motion to dismiss then you will just have to answer just like any other defendant. So once the complaint and answer have been filed the court has said that at least as alleged there is legal sufficiency for the lawsuit, then what generally happens is you proceed into what's called discovery. Discovery is the process by which both sides give up all the information that is relevant to the particular lawsuit. Both the claims that the plaintiff is making and any defenses that the defendant has. Long gone are the days where you can ambush people at trial. Trial is really just the tip of the lawsuit iceberg. For the most part, most of the lawsuit consists of this factual discovery back and forth because we've decided that it is better for the judicial process for people to have all of the relevant information that is necessary to adjudicate these cases before going to trial. Discovery consists of essentially requests from one side to the other and then responses from that side back to the original requesting side. This includes things like interrogatories which are questions that the other side has to answer. It includes document requests where you request a certain category of documents. And unless it's objectionable, the other side must respond by providing all of those documents. You'll often see on TV and movies, people talking about being buried in documents. One way of responding to document requests is to not look at them discreetly and specifically and just giving the other side every document that could be responsive to the request that you have made. So it's often in your best interest to write your document request in such a way that you get the documents that you want but don't create this really huge bourdon from the other side to compile hundreds of thousands or millions of documents as a result of these requests. You need to thread that needle. And of course discovery also consists of depositions which is where you get to question a witness. Witnesses are under oath, they're responses are recorded by a stenographer. Recently I just reviewed the movie The Social Network which is really interesting from my perspective because it is told almost entirely through deposition testimony. And you can imagine that when you're in a lawsuit being able to question witnesses and parties to that lawsuit can be a very, very important tool to find out what happened in any particular legal situation. It's a great way to get evidence and to be able to destroy the other side's legal claims as well. But of course the downside to the factual discovery process is that it is very resource intensive. It takes a ton of time to draft requests, to respond to requests. And then of course to cull through sometimes millions of pages of documents just to look for the things that support your case or support your defense. Discovery is what makes up the vast majority of litigating a lawsuit. So generally speaking, once factual discovery has commenced or has completed, one or both of the parties will file what's called a motion for summary judgment. Now where a motion to dismiss does not attach any evidence whatsoever because it takes the allegations and the complaint as true, a motion for summary judgment can use evidence. You will compile some of the evidence that you gathered through document requests, through interrogatories and through depositions, you will attach that to your motion and it's basically a trial on paper. The motion for summary judgement is a way of ending a lawsuit early without getting to trial. But the downside is that both sides effectively must agree to the evidence that is undisputed. And of course many sides do in fact dispute the evidence at the motion for summary judgment stage. But a judge will take that into consideration and will look at the evidence presented and actually determine if the parties really do dispute the evidence or if the other side can offer contradictory evidence. So assuming that it's the defendant that files a motion for summary judgment and says that even with undisputed facts the lawsuit fails, the plaintiff can respond to by saying legally their case is still sound and that even with these undisputed facts they are entitled to a legal remedy. Or the plaintiff can beat a motion for summary judgment by demonstrating that in fact the facts go different ways, that there's contradictory evidence and that the facts are disputed in such a way that a should not be granted. Now assuming that all motions for summary judgment are denied, that generally leads to pretrial motions and what we call motions in limine which are motions that deal with evidentiary issues. One thing you see in legal movies and legal TV shows all the time is that they have all of these evidentiary disputes in the middle of trial. That's not particularly realistic. For the most part, if you have evidence that A, you think is really, really important and B, you think the other side is going to contest and not stipulate to the admission of that evidence, what you'll want to do is file a pretrial motion to allow that evidence to come in. So for the most part, all of the really important evidentiary issues don't happen in the middle of trial where there's this Perry Mason moment where you're not sure if the judge is going to allow your evidence to come in or not. That's taken care of months before the trial in pretrial motions and motions in limine because if the evidence is really important and you need it to come in, you want to provide as much briefing and as much legal reasoning as to why it should come in. Or legal reasoning as to why the other side's evidence should be kept out because you'll never be able to do as much in oral argument especially on the day of trial. After the pretrial issues are dealt with that leads to trial. Now less than 2% of all filed lawsuits ever get anywhere close to trial. For the most part, most lawsuits are either rejected at the motion stage or they will settle ahead of time long before you get to trial. Because trial is incredibly expensive. But assuming that you get to trial, of course everybody knows about opening statements where each side will explain what facts they intend to bring out in that particular trial. Then you go on to examinations where you conduct the direct examination of your witnesses, the plaintiff goes first. Then each individual witness is cross-examined by the defense. Then the defense witnesses go where the defendants will provide their witnesses who are examined and cross-examined by the plaintiffs. Then there are closing arguments which is the one place where you are allowed to engage in argument. Openings are generally supposed to be argument free, just explaining what kind of facts are gonna come out. Closing arguments is where you are able to tie everything together. And then after closing argument the trier of fact, whether that is a judge or a jury, will render a decision about which side wins or loses. There can be an appeal. On appeal, you're not retrying the facts, the facts are set by the trial court. On appeal the only things that are at issue are discreet legal issues. With a given set of facts the court of appeal will look at the decisions that the trial court made and determine if they were legally correct based on those facts. Of course there are exceptions to this but that's generally what's happening when you appeal. And by the way, I've heard so many times people say, I'm goin to appeal this all the way to the Supreme Court. No, no you're not. The Supreme Court doesn't really review run of the mill lawsuits. The Supreme Court only takes a case if there is a tricky Constitutional question or a question of first impression that deals with federal laws. Odds are, your lawsuit doesn't come anywhere close to those issues, so there's no way you will ever appeal your case to the Supreme Court. It's just not going to happen. So that's how a basic civil lawsuit works. Use this new found knowledge for good, not for evil. Of course, one group of people that are constantly getting sued are tech companies because they can't seem to keep your passwords secure. EDEXCEL GCSE (1CP2) Ethical and legal issues with collection of personal data: Kind: captions Language: en-GB - [Craig] In this video, we discuss the ethical and legal issues with the collection of personal data. (uplifting piano jingle) The rise of computer technology has caused some to question the implications for individuals' privacy. Cameras and surveillance systems have perfected number plate and face recognition so that people can easily be tracked. There are clear benefits such as crime prevention, but it's also open to misuse. Electronic tagging can identify where convicted criminals are with GPS tracking. Black boxes in cars can monitor how people drive for insurance and accident investigation purposes. Mobile phone signals can be tracked, which also allows technology such as Find My iPhone to enable you to find a lost phone or tablet. In addition, GPS technology is used to automatically tag date, time and location where photographs are taken, and social networking tools allow this data to easily be shared with friends and family. Schools will monitor internet and computer activity of their students, and this logging also extends to the workplace, where work patterns and phone calls are routinely recorded. Modern browsers record a history of sites visited and have options to retain passwords and credit card details to make browsing easier. This may give rise to piracy issues if other computer users access this data. Big data and data mining is slightly outside the scope of the course but is fascinating. It's a process of looking for patterns and trends from very large data sets, which can be used to make strategic business decisions as well as targeting advertising. Data is now a very valuable commodity. There are therefore many avenues to explore when considering privacy. Sir Tim Berners-Lee, inventor of the World Wide Web, criticised moves by legislators in the UK and the US, which he saw as an assault on the privacy of web users. He has criticised the UK's Investigatory Powers Act, saying that the idea that all ISPs should be required to spy on citizens and hold the data for six months is appalling. In the United States, he is concerned that the principle of net neutrality, which treats all internet traffic equally, could be watered down by the Trump administration and the Federal Communications Commission. The web's creator also said he was shocked by the direction the US Congress and Senate had taken when they voted to scrap laws preventing internet service providers from selling users' data. He said privacy online was as important as the trust between a doctor and a patient. We're talking about a human right. My ability to communicate with people on the web and to go to websites I want without being spied on is really, really crucial. An increasing range of smart devices in our homes are voice-activated, and they're capable of listening in on our daily activities. Some people argue that the government are collecting valuable information for counterterrorism operations using this technology. So should smart devices be allowed to continually record and listen? There are several issues associated with the collection and use of personal data, which you are expected to know about for the exam. We're going to run through them now. The first is privacy. So consider who has access to your data and how is this access controlled? Is the data private? The second is the issue of ownership. You may have supplied a lot of your personal data to a company or an online platform, but who actually owns the data held about you? Well under current data protection legislation in the UK, you own the vast majority of your own personal data, regardless of who may currently hold it. This is the concept in law of the owner of a data, which is you, versus the stewards or the protectors of the data, which will be the people who hold it. We also have a right to erasure, your legal right to have your personal data removed from any system you've supplied it to. The third issue is that of consent. How can data be collected on you? Reinforced data protection laws in this country, with the recent implementation of GDPR, require organisations to ask for positive consent or opt-in. It is no longer legally sufficient for them to collect data on you automatically or for them to get you to simply tick a box that says I don't want my data used. You now have to positively consent or opt in. And this opt-in message must be in plain, simple English and must be obvious to the user. The next issue to be aware of is that of misuse. With vast amounts of your personal data now supplied to businesses and online platforms, we have to think about how this could potentially be misused. Could these websites be hacked? What measures do they have in place? What preventative systems do they have to protect you? Could you be subject to phishing scams if your information is hacked? And finally, and also spanning across all of these issues, is the underlying concept of data protection. How is your actual data protected? What are your legal rights? And this all leads to what is known as data protection law or legislation. Over the last few years, data protection legislation has become incredibly robust in both the UK and throughout mainland Europe with the implementation of the General Data Protection Regulation. And this is something we're going to take a look at in a little more detail in the next section of this video. The Data Protection Act 2018 is an updated piece of UK legislation which is designed to protect an individual's personal data. Prior to 2018, it already provided a great deal of protection to UK citizens, but it has since been updated and made even more robust by fully implementing all of the General Data Protection Regulations set down by the European Union. It provides various rights and guarantees under law, and these are known as its basic principles. The first is fair, lawful and transparent processing. Personal data must be processed lawfully, fairly and in a transparent manner in relation to the data subject. This means that all data controllers must only process data for the purpose they acquired it and with consideration of the data subject's rights. The next principle is purpose limitation. Data must only be collected for specified, explicit and legitimate purposes and not further processed in a manner that is incompatible with those purposes. The only exception to this purpose is relating to public interest and scientific or historical research. The next process is data minimisation. This means only holding as much data about a person as is necessary. Data must be adequate, relevant and limited to what is necessary in relation to the purposes for which they're being processed. For example, if you are collecting data to post a catalogue, you only need the person's name and address. You do not need their date of birth or gender. It's not relevant. The next principle is accuracy. The data you collect must be accurate and where necessary, kept up to date. Every reasonable step must be taken to ensure that personal data that isn't accurate is corrected. To fulfil this principle, you must update data if a customer notifies you of a change. You must also erase the data if it's no longer necessary. And under the regulation, data subjects have the right to rectification, and this must be fulfilled within a month. The next principle is data retention periods. Data you hold must be kept in a form which permits identification of data subjects for no longer than is necessary for the purposes for which the personal data is processed. You may store it for longer for archiving purposes in the public interest, scientific or historical research purposes. that of data security. The data you hold must be processed in a manner that ensures appropriate security of the personal data, including protection against unauthorised or unlawful processing and against accidental loss, destruction or damage using appropriate technical or organisational measures. The last principle is accountability. Now, accountability is a new principle in the Data Protection Act so it can comply with GDPR. In order to do this, data controllers must be able to prove that their data protection measures are sufficient. They must have appropriate technical and organisational procedures, which include suitable privacy policies and keeping sufficient records of their processing activities. Another piece of legislation you have to be aware of is the Computer Misuse Act of 1990. And this is an example of a piece of legislation which had to be created in order to keep up with technology. This act makes it illegal to make any unauthorised access to data with the intent to commit further offences or with the intent to modify data; e.g., via viruses. By bringing in this act, there's a whole series of online activities which are now illegal. Some examples are on the screen like the planting of viruses, hacking, data theft and fraud. Now you also need to know about the Privacy and Electronic Communications Regulations, especially in regards to internet cookies. So typically, a cookie is a small text file with ID tags that is stored in your computer, and cookies can be created when you use your browser to visit a website. They're typically used to keep track of your movements within the website and can help by resuming where you left off when you revisit the site, remembering your registered login, remembering theme selections, remembering preferences, and customising your experience in various other ways. By law, any website operator using cookies must tell the user cookies are there, explain what the cookies are doing, and obtain the user's consent to store cookies on their device. So in summary, we've provided some information which was gone through in this video. Pause it now and take some notes. An additional summary here of the Data Protection Act, the Computer Misuse Act and the Privacy and Electronics Regulations are provided. Pause the video and take some more notes.

- DO NOT Pay Debt Collectors | How to Handle Debt When It’s Gone to Collections: Kind: captions Language: en-US How should you handle debt once&nbsp; it has gone into collections?&nbsp;&nbsp; Now, not to give away the ending, but I'm&nbsp; telling you right now that paying it is&nbsp;&nbsp; quite literally the very last thing that you&nbsp; should do when it comes to debt collectors. Hi, I'm Laurie-Anne, co-founder of Dow Janes, and&nbsp; if you are dealing with the stress of how having a&nbsp;&nbsp; debt collector chasing you for money, don't worry.&nbsp; I am going to tell you everything that you need to&nbsp;&nbsp; know -- what a debt collector cannot do, what&nbsp; your rights are, and how you should handle debt&nbsp;&nbsp; collectors. If you want to learn more about how&nbsp; to save, invest, and make the most of your money,&nbsp;&nbsp; be sure to subscribe and hit the bell&nbsp; so you don't miss out on future videos. Now, let's dive in what happens&nbsp; when your debt goes to collections. Well, one of two things happens&nbsp; when a debt goes into collections.&nbsp;&nbsp; Either option one, the creditor that you owe&nbsp; money to -- so, say your credit card issuer or&nbsp;&nbsp; your mortgage lender -- when the lender&nbsp; thinks that you're behind on payments,&nbsp;&nbsp; the creditor might use its own debt collectors&nbsp; to follow up and collect the debt from you.&nbsp;&nbsp; Or they may hire a debt collection&nbsp; agency or a law firm to take it on. Option number two is that a different&nbsp; company actually buys your debt from the&nbsp;&nbsp; original creditor and now they are trying&nbsp; to collect on the money that you owe.&nbsp;&nbsp; In this case, the original lender probably&nbsp; already received some sort of insurance money&nbsp;&nbsp; and sold your debt for pennies on the dollar. Now, before I give you some tips&nbsp; for dealing with debt collectors,&nbsp;&nbsp; I wanna make sure you know, what debt collectors&nbsp; can and cannot do and how to make sure that you&nbsp;&nbsp; are not being scammed and what your rights&nbsp; are when it comes to debt collections. So, the first thing I want you to know&nbsp; is that debt collectors are only allowed&nbsp;&nbsp; restricted contact. They can only&nbsp; call you between the hours of 8:00 AM&nbsp;&nbsp; and 9:00 PM. And they're not&nbsp; allowed to call you at work.&nbsp;&nbsp; So if you get a call outside of these hours, then&nbsp; you might be dealing with a debt collection scam. The second thing I want you to know is&nbsp; that you can request a callback number.&nbsp;&nbsp; Just like any other legitimate business, a debt&nbsp; collection agency should be able to provide you&nbsp;&nbsp; with company information, including a number to&nbsp; call them back. When you get contacted by them,&nbsp;&nbsp; write down as much information about the company&nbsp; as possible, including its name and address.&nbsp;&nbsp; This is important to see if they&nbsp; are legally operating in your state. The third thing I want you to know&nbsp; is that debt collectors cannot lie&nbsp;&nbsp; or harass you, or just generally be toxic people. Debt collectors cannot make you pay more than&nbsp; you owe or threaten you with arrest, jail time,&nbsp;&nbsp; property liens, or wage garnishment. If you don't&nbsp; pay, they do not have the power to do that. So if&nbsp;&nbsp; they say, otherwise, hang up. Wage garnishment may&nbsp; be legal in your state, but your debt collector&nbsp;&nbsp; will need to take you to court first. And if a&nbsp; debt collector is posing as police or threatens&nbsp;&nbsp; to arrest you, then there's a good chance&nbsp; that this is a scam and you could report them&nbsp;&nbsp; and the threat to the Federal Trade Commission&nbsp; and the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau. The last thing I want you to know about your&nbsp; rights is that you can stop a debt collector&nbsp;&nbsp; from contacting you under the Fair Debt Collection&nbsp; Practices Act. If you notify the collection agency&nbsp;&nbsp; in writing to stop communicating with you, then&nbsp; they can only contact you again to advise you&nbsp;&nbsp; on one of two things: 1) that they're going&nbsp; to stop trying to collect from you or 2) that&nbsp;&nbsp; they intend to take action against you, such as&nbsp; filing a lawsuit. And this law applies even if&nbsp;&nbsp; you do own the debt. So you do not have to just&nbsp; surrender to the fact that you're gonna be called&nbsp;&nbsp; repeatedly by debt collectors. Notify them in&nbsp; writing if you don't want to hear from any more. Now that you understand your rights and&nbsp; how to watch out for potential scams,&nbsp;&nbsp; let's talk about how you should actually handle&nbsp; it when the debt collector starts ringing you up. Number one, don't admit to the debt.&nbsp;&nbsp; I know I might sound like a fat cat lawyer here,&nbsp; but seriously, just don't admit to the debt,&nbsp;&nbsp; especially not on the first phone call. Some&nbsp; debts come with a statute of limitations,&nbsp;&nbsp; meaning after a certain period of time, collectors&nbsp; legally can't win a court order for repayment. And so if you admit that the debt is yours,&nbsp; you may actually reset the clock on that&nbsp;&nbsp; statute of limitations. So don't confirm&nbsp; the debt, especially on your first contact,&nbsp;&nbsp; until you get more facts, even&nbsp; if you know the debt is yours. So, point number two is you want to get&nbsp; the facts straight. Without admitting that&nbsp;&nbsp; the debt is yours, get information on the debt&nbsp; itself. Ask who the original creditor was, the&nbsp;&nbsp; original debt amount, and how much is owed. And&nbsp; the more details the debt collector can provide,&nbsp;&nbsp; the better. If the statute of limitations has&nbsp; expired, the debt collector can no longer sue&nbsp;&nbsp; you to recoup the debt. Plus, when your debt&nbsp; has been sold, there's often misinformation&nbsp;&nbsp; about the debt itself or who owes it, so you&nbsp; wanna make sure that all of that is accurate. Point number three, don't&nbsp; be emotionally manipulated. A lot of people feel guilty about their debt&nbsp; and debt collectors prey on that emotion.&nbsp;&nbsp; They create a sense of urgency that hits you&nbsp; right where you're sensitive. We always tell&nbsp;&nbsp; people that they have to learn how to deal with&nbsp; the difference between money facts and money&nbsp;&nbsp; feelings. So think about where your emotions and&nbsp; attitude towards debt and money have come from,&nbsp;&nbsp; and then release yourself from negative emotions&nbsp; that are holding you back. If you find yourself&nbsp;&nbsp; on the phone with a debt collector,&nbsp; don't let them make you feel bad, okay?&nbsp;&nbsp; And don't pay or promise payment or give any&nbsp; payment information at this time. You sensing&nbsp;&nbsp; a theme in this video? Like I said, paying is&nbsp; literally the last thing that you're going to do.&nbsp;&nbsp; You want to just ask for information on the&nbsp; debt and say that you'll be in touch later. Number four, dispute any errors with the debt. If you find any erroneous information with&nbsp; the claim, dispute it. Mail a letter to the&nbsp;&nbsp; debt collector stating the amount that&nbsp; they're saying you owe is incorrect.&nbsp;&nbsp; You should also ask for proof of the debt&nbsp; collector's claim that you owe money,&nbsp;&nbsp; such as copy of a credit card bill, and be&nbsp; sure to keep a copy of the letter yourself.&nbsp;&nbsp; The Consumer Financial Protection Bureau&nbsp; offers a sample dispute letter on its website,&nbsp;&nbsp; which I'll link to down below. If you and&nbsp; the collector cannot settle the dispute,&nbsp;&nbsp; you may want to bring in a third party arbitrator&nbsp; to weigh the evidence, and if things get really&nbsp;&nbsp; hairy, you may want to consider hiring an&nbsp; attorney. Make sure that you have records of&nbsp;&nbsp; everything that you've been discussing with the&nbsp; collector in case it reaches this point. It's&nbsp;&nbsp; always better to communicate via email or mail so&nbsp; you have a copy of your conversations in writing. The fifth step: you're going to decide if you&nbsp; want to pay off this debt or not. This may sound&nbsp;&nbsp; a little odd. We often feel like we have a moral&nbsp; obligation to pay our debts. But the truth about&nbsp;&nbsp; how debt works is that the lender does take on a&nbsp; certain amount of risk when they lend out money.&nbsp;&nbsp; This is part of why they charge interest; it's to&nbsp; cover the cost of debts that have gone bad. So,&nbsp;&nbsp; you may have your own relationship to debt.&nbsp; You may want to pay off this debt, but it may&nbsp;&nbsp; actually not be in your best financial interest to&nbsp; do so. When a debt goes into collections, the main&nbsp;&nbsp; impact that it has on you is damage to your credit&nbsp; score, and any debt that has already gone into&nbsp;&nbsp; collections has already done that damage. And&nbsp; it's going to continue to have a negative impact&nbsp;&nbsp; on your credit score for about seven years,&nbsp; until it falls off of your credit report. Now, in some cases you may be able to negotiate&nbsp; the removal of this derogatory mark on your credit&nbsp;&nbsp; report as part of paying a settlement on the&nbsp; debt. But if you have a debt that's gone into&nbsp;&nbsp; collections and it's close to being seven years&nbsp; old, it will likely fall off your credit report&nbsp;&nbsp; soon. And it may not be worth paying. It's not&nbsp; going to improve your credit score at this point&nbsp;&nbsp; to clear it off because you've had that mark on&nbsp; your report for so long. And if it's going to fall&nbsp;&nbsp; off and stop damaging your credit report, you may&nbsp; be better off just waiting it out, especially if&nbsp;&nbsp; you have any current debts that you're struggling&nbsp; with. So I want you to focus first on maintaining&nbsp;&nbsp; good standing with the active debts that you&nbsp; have before paying off debts in collections. Number six, negotiate the debt. If you do decide to pay it at the end of the day,&nbsp; whether it's a creditor or a collections agency,&nbsp;&nbsp; reaching out to you, all they want is some money.&nbsp; And usually they paid a lot less for the debt&nbsp;&nbsp; than the amount that you actually owe on it,&nbsp; so they might be willing to consider either&nbsp;&nbsp; lowering the debt so you can pay it all off&nbsp; in one lump sum or setting up a payment plan&nbsp;&nbsp; that you can reasonably manage. Make&nbsp; sure that you negotiate and always,&nbsp;&nbsp; always, always get any agreement that you come&nbsp; to in writing before you take the final step. Step seven: pay down the debt. Now, at long last,&nbsp; after you have done all of the previous steps,&nbsp;&nbsp; it's finally time to pay up. If you&nbsp; have decided to pay off this debt,&nbsp;&nbsp; how you go about this will depend on&nbsp; what you worked out with the collector. - The Fake Courtroom Debt Collection Scam: Kind: captions Language: en Debt Verdict, DV-07, title the fake courtroom debt collection scam category exposed channel verdict. They built a courtroom inside their office. They hung a seal on the wall. They put a man in a black robe behind a raised desk and they told people they were being tried for their debts. None of it was real, but the fear was, the payments were, and the scam worked for years until the real courts found out. This is the story of one of the most brazen debt collection frauds in American history. To understand how this scam worked, you need to understand something about debt in America. According to the Federal Reserve Bank of New York, total household debt in the United States reached 17.69 trillion dollars by the end of 2023. Credit cards alone accounted for over 1.13 trillion dollars. Behind every one of those dollars is a person who owes money and a multi-billion dollar industry that exists to collect it. The debt collection industry in America generates over 20 billion dollars in annual revenue. There are roughly 7,000 collection agencies operating across the country. Most of them follow the law, but some of them have discovered that fear is more profitable than compliance. The Fair Debt Collection Practices Act, the FDCPA, was signed into law in 1977. It exists for one reason, to stop debt collectors from using deception, threats, and harassment to collect money. Under section 807 of the FDCPA, it is explicitly illegal for a debt collector to use any false, deceptive, or misleading representation in connection with the collection of any debt. That includes falsely representing that they are attorneys, that documents are legal forms, or that any communication is from a court. The penalties for violating the FDCPA can include statutory damages of up to $1,000 per violation for individuals, actual damages, and attorney's fees. In class action cases, penalties can reach up to $500,000 or 1% of a collector's net worth. Those rules exist, but rules only work if someone enforces them. The scheme was disturbingly simple, a debt collection operation, and there have been multiple documented cases of this type of fraud across different states would set up what looked like a courtroom inside their office space. The room would be decorated with official-looking elements, a wooden bench elevated like a judge's seat, a seal or emblem on the wall, flags, a witness stand, rows of chairs for an audience that would never come. The collectors would wear suits, the person behind the bench would wear a dark robe, some operations even had a person playing a bailiff, standing by the door with an authoritative look. Then the debtors would be summoned. They would receive letters or phone calls telling them they were required to appear for a legal proceeding regarding their outstanding debt. The language was crafted to sound official. Words like hearing, proceeding, judgment, and court order were scattered throughout the communications. When the debtor arrived, they would be ushered into the fake courtroom. The man in the robe who was not a judge had no legal authority and in some cases had no legal training whatsoever, would preside over a sham proceeding. The debtor would be told they owed a specific amount. They would be told that failure to pay would result in wage garnishment, asset seizure, or even arrest. None of these threats were real. But the people sitting in that chair didn't know that. Many of them were low-income individuals. Many had limited education. Many had never been inside a real courtroom. They had no frame of reference to know the difference between a legitimate legal proceeding and a theatrical performance designed to terrify them into handing over money. And it worked. People paid. They paid amounts they couldn't afford, sometimes by borrowing from family or taking out additional loans. They paid because they believed a judge had ordered them to. They paid because they were afraid of going to jail. The Federal Trade Commission has pursued numerous cases against fraudulent debt collection operations over the years. In 2015, the FTC in the state of New York shut down a massive operation run by a company called William Scott and Associates, which was using fake courtroom-style tactics, threats of arrest, and impersonation of law enforcement to collect debts. The operation had collected millions from consumers using illegal threats and misrepresentations. The FTC obtained a court order banning the principles from the debt collection industry entirely. In another landmark case, the FTC took action against AMG services and its affiliated companies, which had been engaged in deceptive lending and collection practices. The case went all the way to the Supreme Court in 2021, AMG Capital Management v. FTC, where the court ruled on the FTC's authority to seek monetary relief. The Consumer Financial Protection Bureau has also been active. In 2016, the CFPB took action against Encore Capital Group and its subsidiary Midland Credit Management, one of the largest debt buyers in the United States. For using deceptive tactics, including filing lawsuits to collect debts, they couldn't actually prove consumers owed. The settlement required Encore to pay up to $42 million in restitution to consumers. These cases reveal a pattern. The debt collection industry, at its worst, operates on a simple principle. Most people won't fight back. Most people won't hire a lawyer. Most people won't file a complaint. Most people will just pay, even when they don't legally owe the money because the fear of legal consequences is overwhelming. The fake courtroom is just the most theatrical version of this strategy. But the same underlying tactic, using fear, confusion, and false authority to extract payment, runs through thousands of collection operations across the country. Here's what every person in America needs to know about debt collection. Under the FDCPA, you have specific legally protected rights. A debt collector must provide written validation of the debt within five days of first contacting you. You have 30 days to dispute the debt in writing. If you dispute, the collector must stop all collection activity until they provide verification. No debt collector can threaten you with arrest. In the United States, you cannot be jailed for failing to pay a consumer debt. That has been true since the federal government effectively abolished debtor's prisons in 1833. Any collector who tells you otherwise is breaking the law. No debt collector can impersonate a lawyer, a judge, a law enforcement officer, or a government official. No debt collector can claim that documents are legal forms when they are not. No debt collector can threaten legal action they do not intend to take or cannot legally take. If a debt collector violates the FDCPA, you can file a complaint with the FTC at ftc.gov, with the CFPB at consumerfinance.gov, and with your state attorney general's office. You can also sue the collector in federal or state court within one year of the violation. The fake courtroom scam works because it exploits something universal, the fear of authority. When someone in a robe behind an elevated bench tells you that you owe money, every instinct in your body says comply. That instinct is what these scammers count on. But authority has to be earned. It has to be verified. Real courts send real summons through official channels. Real judges are listed in public records. Real legal proceedings happen in government buildings, not in rented office spaces with plywood benches. The debt collection industry handles billions of dollars every year. Most of it is legitimate. But the fraction that isn't can destroy lives, not because the debts are real, but because the fear is. Knowledge is the only defense. If you know your rights, a fake courtroom is just a room with furniture. If you don't, it's a trap. If you've ever dealt with aggressive debt collectors or been threatened with fake legal action, drop your story in the comments. Subscribe for more stories of debt and justice.

- How Do I Sell My Coins? Beginner's Guide to Selling Your Coin Collection: Kind: captions Language: en How to sell your coins. That's a really important thing to know if you've got coins and you want to sell them, right? Well, it's not quite so easy as just finding some random person and selling it sometimes because there's a lot of things to take into account. But today, I'm going to walk you through some basics of how to sell your coins, some different ways to do it, and kind of what I recommend personally, but I'm going to give you a lot of different options. Now, before you just start going out and trying to sell any particular thing that you have, there's some really useful information you need to know, right? Like what's the condition of it? How desirable is it? And what's it worth? So, probably the first thing you need to find out is what is the coin. You need to be able to define what it is. We're going to talk a little more about this coming up, but I highly recommend the US Red Book of Coins to help identify which coin you have and learn tons of great information about it. But if you're still struggling with it, I recommend Google image search. Just take a picture of it and let Google help. And after that, it's important to figure out which one it is. There's a big difference between a 1909 penny, a 1909 S penny, a 1909 VDB penny, and a 1909 SVDB penny. But they were all pennies made in 1909. That's not just what type of coin it is, but which particular one it is, what year, what mint mark, or maybe even a specific type of coin for that particular year. Maybe there were multiple different designs done for that year. anything special about it that might make it more valuable than something else very similar to it. All that's pertinent information you need to find. And that's important to figure out because all the coins I mentioned there are pennies from 1909, but they have different rarities and different values in different grades. And that's where it gets kind of complicated with coins. You see, you have a particular coin that might be worth one thing in one grade, but if it's an exceptionally nice example, depending on where it falls on the scale, it could be worth a lot more or a lot less. And so, that next step is condition. Figuring out what the condition of the coin is. Because you might have a coin that's pretty darn easy to get, like a 1936D quarter, that in lower condition ranges is just worth its silver value. But if you have a 1936D in a mintstate range, that's a completely different story. Kind of like this 1936D right here that's in a gorgeous mintstate range. But there's one more thing we have to figure out about it, and that's whether it's real. Because this 1936D quarter is actually fake. Figuring out if a coin is real or not is easier said than done. There's a lot to determining whether a coin is real, and that would be an entire video series on its own. The easiest way for you to figure out if a coin that it does have some significant rarity to it is real would be to take it to a local coin shop or to a local coin show where you can talk to someone who buys and sells coins on a regular basis and they can authenticate the coin for you at least to a certain degree. They can let you know whether it's solidly real or not or whether you should probably seek a more professional approach like sending it to a third party grading service to determine authenticity. And that leads me to the last point that we need to talk about before we start talking about value and selling your coin. And that's whether the coin has been graded by a professional thirdparty grading service or not. When coin collectors talk about graded coins, they're talking about stuff like this. coins that are in holders from a legitimate third-party grading service that has not only authenticated the coin and it described what it is on the holder, but they've also assigned a numerical grade between 1 and 70 to determine just how nice that coin really is. As opposed to something like this that's not graded or ungraded or sometimes referred to as raw and the coin has not been professionally graded by a third party grading service. Now, sometimes people do put them in flips or something like that and write a grade on it, but those grades should be taken with a grain of salt until they've actually been graded by a third party grading service because most people aren't very good at grading. I want to be clear, most coins are not worth sending off for grading because of the cost of grading as well as the value of the coin in reality. But if it has already been graded by a third-party grading service, that makes your life so much easier. you can simply read what's on the coin to figure out what it is as well as already have a grade and you're done. But if the coin is raw, you do have those additional steps of putting this all together. There are a few resources that can help you out with this process. I would recommend to anyone who's interested in owning, collecting, or selling coins to have a copy of the Red Book. This is the 2026 Red Book of Coins, and it's gotten better this year. This is the first time I've ever told people, go out and buy a new red book. It's actually really good and they changed some stuff up this year, but this book has information on all US coins that you can really look up and find out a lot of wonderful information. They have a mega red book that costs more money that gives you even more information, but this will suffice for getting the values of the coins in different grades on a retail side, not on a uh not on a wholesale side, but on a retail side to help you better understand the value in general of your coins. However, if you do want to sell your coins, it's probably good to understand wholesale value. So, in that case, the blue book actually reigns supreme because that's going to be kind of the base level value of coins because a lot of values shown in the red book will be significantly higher than you will ever receive out of a particular coin. And so the blue book actually accounts for that and shows not a retail side, but the lower wholesale side that's more realistic of what a dealer is probably going to pay you for your coins and give you a better perspective on what you should expect when you actually sell the coins in helping you figure out the values of those coins. As I said, you have to figure out grade. That's a great opportunity to pick up something like the ANA grading standards book. But if you do spring for that mega red book that's significantly more money, it's actually got pictures of the coins in different grades as well. So that actually could suffice instead of buying the ANA grading standards book. But if you just want a free option to figure out what your coins are possibly worth, I would recommend going to PCGS Coinfax. They have a wonderful section on there where you can figure out what the condition of the coin is with their photo grading as well as they have on the coin facts page for any particular coin information about what the coins are worth in specific grades. However, those values are set high for graded coins. So, it is a strict retail value on graded coins and doesn't really necessarily reflect the actual values even of the graded coins. So, it's going to be exceptionally high if they're not graded. But if you look at those numbers below the price guide, that's something that really is helpful for graded coin pricing because those are actual sold comps from NGC and PCGS examples. But if you click this little button right here that says show eBay, guess what? You can see sales from eBay as well as major auction houses that are already shown. But the best way ever to figure out what your coin is worth is to look up and see what they've actually sold for at auction or as a buy it now on eBay and other platforms. You can do that by looking up the coin on eBay. Ignore what people are asking for it. Do not just stop there. Go to the side of the page, filter by sold listings, and you can see what coins have actually sold for. And that gives you a much clearer perspective on what they're actually worth. Okay, so you've got all that together. You know what the coin is, you know the general condition, you know whether it's graded or whether it's a raw coin or ungraded coin. And in the cases of high rarity, you've sought outside counsel to see if it's actually real or not. So that when you sell your coin, you don't have problems of your listing getting taken down or people thinking that you don't know what you're doing when you try and sell a fake coin. So now, what do you do? Well, there are a lot of options with selling coins. They each have their pros and cons to them. I'm going to try and go through this list pretty quick. There is the option to sell it privately. You can do something like Craigslist, Facebook Marketplace, and try and find a buyer. The difficulty with this is you really don't know who you're dealing with. There are a lot of people out there that are less than reputable, I guess, is a good way to put it. And um depending on how much money you're talking, meeting somebody in a parking lot to sell them a coin can be kind of sketchy. I'm just putting that out there. It's in my opinion the best way to profit on the coins that you have because you're selling it at a price that you agree on and it can be a high retail value, but you are involving a lot of risk as well. Also, finding that one person who wants that coin and them being in your area might not be so easy as well, especially if it's not a significantly valuable or rare coin. Most people will look at it and say, "Well, I can buy that somewhere else just as easily at like a coin shop or something." So, it might not motivate them at a high retail number. Unless it is something that's significant, or you just find that right person. It's your job to find the guy. Not only list the item on there, find the person, meet them, hope they follow through and actually show up and don't rob you in the process, and then you've sold a coin possibly. But the next option is one that I actually do recommend and I've used before and that's different Facebook groups for buying and selling coins. There are Facebook groups that are devoted to particular types of coins as well as just buying and selling coins in general. You want to find a group that actually fits the niche of the coin that you have if you can. That's usually the best way to do it. You want to set it up within their guidelines. is make sure you read their rules and make sure that you know what you're properly describing the coin and then you're able to list the coin on there and have it sold. You avoid that risk of having to meet a random stranger somewhere and that can make it a lot better. Also, there are usually pretty experienced people in those groups who might be able to help you if you're a little bit off on the coin. Maybe you overgraded it or maybe you undergraded it or maybe it's something that is completely different than what you think it is. There are people who are usually pretty knowledgeable in those groups who can chime in and help you out with that. Just be open to the criticism because they might be a little blunt with saying that's not what this is. But if that's not what it is, it's a great learning opportunity to figure out what it is. Another great online method in my opinion is eBay. If you've got a coin and you put it on eBay, guess what? Everybody in the world who uses eBay can see what that coin is and can buy it from you. That's a great marketplace that a lot of coin collectors use to try and find coins they're looking for. The difficulty is on eBay, you are fighting against every other person who puts something up on there if you do it as a buy it now option and you're basically waiting until somebody chooses your coin over somebody else's. It's also a little difficult because you got to make sure the price and the description of the coin are right and the grade that you've priced it off of because if it's not, you're throwing a coin up on there as a buy it now is competing against every other one on there and that can take some time, especially if it's not right. But having a coin set up as a buy it now with offers available can actually be a beneficial thing. You set it up one time and eventually somebody comes along and they make an offer or they just buy the coin and then you ship it to them. And there's another side to eBay as well that's guaranteed to sell your coin no matter what. And that's eBay auctions. If you get good pictures of the coin, if you describe the coin correctly and you at least get a pretty basic concept of what it is in the description of the coin, like in the title, it's going to sell probably for what the market value of it is. I know a lot of people are saying I I don't believe that auctions are risky. But realistically, it's going to go for at least close to wholesale because no coin dealer, trust me, there are plenty of them watching eBay consistently. No coin dealer out there is going to let a coin go too low just because it was being missed by other collectors. And I sell coins at $1 start auctions on a regular basis whenever I sell coins on eBay because I know somebody's going to find it. As long as I've described it right and I've gotten really clear pictures of it, it's probably going to do just fine. The downside to eBay on both the buy it now and the auction side is the fees. The fees can really eat into your value there. It can really cut into what you get out of the coin. On top of that, you've put a lot of work into listing it and shipping it. So, you know, there's some effort you've put into it, not only researching it, but all that other stuff just to have a less than wholesale value sometimes because of all those fees. Now, the next to last one I'm going to mention is really only good if you have high value coins, and that's going to be major auction houses. I do recommend major auction houses for really high value coins that are already graded or ones that are worthy of grading because of their rarity or their high condition. Uh, but that's really going to be limited to exceptionally rare coins and ones that really have some value to them because auction houses have fees and auction houses are going to get their fees no matter what your coin sells for. I have sold a coin at an auction house that when it sold, I owed the auction house money because the coin sold for less than the cost of the fees I owed them. So, I literally paid them to sell my coin and got nothing out of it. It can happen. You want to make sure the coins you send are the right ones. And if you're wondering whether a coin's worthy of going to an auction house, you can actually speak with the auction house. Uh they have ways you can contact them and tell them what you have and they will let you know whether it's something they're interested in. So between that and researching what you have, you can determine whether all the fees of grading and the auction house fees and the effort of shipping it to them and all that kind of stuff is going to be worth it probably in the long run. But as I said, I don't really think most coins are worth sending off to an auction house. You're really talking about coins that are at least close to $1,000 and up in my opinion for auction house submissions. The last method of selling a coin is one that I really do believe in. And I know people are going to say you're just trying to get people to walk into your coin shop because there's a sign about it right there. But I really do believe in going to coin shops. And I really do believe for most people, it's going to be the easiest and best way to sell most collections of coins. I like to remind people in anything I talk about that time is money. If you're putting in time to make money, you need to take that into account. The amount of time it takes to research coins to figure out whether they're even coins that have a retail side to them, whether they're even retailable coins, can be quite an endeavor in and of itself. For example, if you don't know which wheat pennies are valuable and you spend an entire day going through mixed circulated wheat pennies to try and find some valuable dates, you could literally spend all day with no results over a simple bulk range where the best coin you might find might realistically sell for 15. dealers are going to know automatically which coins are ones they can actually retail, which ones are bulk, and which ones they can recommend you take to the coin store or spend the next time that you go to the grocery store. We can look at coins in a massive amount, just even a pile of them, sort through them really quick, and say, "This is what I can pay." Because we know pricing on coins. We know which ones are rare and which ones aren't. We know the general pricing of bulk. We know the pricing of nicer coins and we know which coins we can pay well on right off the bat without you even doing much research on it. Now, researching before you go to a coin shop is beneficial because there are some dealers out there that are less than reputable as well as there are dealers out there that might not know something about a coin that you do. And if you bring it to their attention, it could actually make them pay you more. Now, in most cases, the dealer's probably going to know more about the coin than you do. I'm going to let you know that now. But in some cases, I have had some customers point out something like, "This coin looks weird." And I've looked at it and said, "Wow, that's actually a pretty cool error. I can pay more for that coin." But apart from that, taking it to a dealer, they can at least give you an idea of what they would pay for it on the wholesale side and give you an idea of what it's worth. Now, if you're wary of whether a particular dealer is giving you a good or bad deal, it doesn't hurt to shop it around to multiple dealers. It does there's nothing wrong with not accepting the offer, taking it somewhere else, and seeing what the offers between those different dealers are. Coin pricing is more complicated than just saying this is what the coin's worth. Coins don't work that way. So, different dealers are probably going to offer you different amounts of money. They might also have a different market where their customers pay a little more on a particular thing versus something else and the other dealer might be able to pay more on that other thing because they have a stronger market on that. So, that is a thing as well. But overall, dealers are probably going to be pretty close on what they offer you. Um, and if you get one that's significantly lower, then guess what? You can just not sell it to that dealer and consider the other offers that you've got. But, as I said, selling to a dealer, you cut out a lot of time. and you cut out a lot of research. You have people who already know how to grade coins looking at them. You have people who already know a lot about coins that are sorting them out for you and they know what they're looking for and they know what they can pay and what they can sell them for and that saves a lot of headache. So for me, even before I worked at a coin shop, I have sold plenty of coins to dealers. In fact, that's why I started working at Foley Coin Shop is because I was a customer before I was an employee. I would come into the shop one or two times a week from the day they opened buying and selling coins. All of that being said, I hope this video has helped you out in thinking about the pros and cons of different ways to sell your coins, as well as the stuff you need to do before you even get to selling them so you can make sure you get the best value out of it and actually sell it at the right price for what it actually is. If this video did help you out, I'd greatly appreciate a like. - How Does Provenance Affect An Item’s Value?: Kind: captions Language: en Have you ever looked at an old unique item and wondered about its journey through time? Who owned it and what stories it could tell? That hidden history, the life story of an object, often holds the key to its true worth. Provenence, which refers to the documented history of ownership of an object, significantly increases an item's value by establishing its authenticity, confirming its rarity, and highlighting its historical or cultural importance. This verified lineage provides a crucial layer of trust and desiraability for collectors and institutions alike. The primary reason Providence boosts value is its power to authenticate. In a market where fakes and reproductions are common, a clear chain of ownership supported by historical records like sales receipts, exhibition cataloges, or letters removes doubt. This verified history assures potential buyers that they are acquiring a genuine piece, directly reducing the risk associated with its purchase. Furthermore, providence can link an item to notable figures, significant events, or important collections, imbuing it with a narrative that elevates it beyond its material qualities. To establish or verify provenence, you should meticulously trace the item's ownership back as far as possible. This involves gathering all available documentation such as bills of sale, old photographs with family members and the item, exhibition records, and academic publications that reference the piece. For items like antique furniture or fine art, look for dealer labels, artist signatures, or gallery stickers, and research the history of those businesses or individuals. Always organize these documents carefully, creating a clear timeline to present the item's journey. An intriguing aspect of providence is how it can sometimes transform a relatively common object into a priceless artifact. Imagine a simple baseball bat used in a local game versus one confirmed to have been swung by a sports legend in a historic moment. The physical objects might be similar, but the bat with documented use by the legend with a clear provenence carries an immeasurable premium due to its connection to history. This is often seen with items that have a connection to cultural icons or pivotal historical events where the story itself becomes the most valuable attribute. Ultimately, understanding and establishing an item's provenence provides peace of mind for both buyers and sellers, confirming the object's legitimacy and often revealing a captivating narrative. This documented history transforms a simple possession into a tangible link to the past, greatly enhancing its market appeal and ensuring its place in a collection while allowing its story to continue. - How Does Provenance Affect The Value Of Rare Books? - The Collectibles Guide: Kind: captions Language: en How does providence affect the value of rare books? Have you ever wondered why some rare books are worth a fortune while others are not? The answer often lies in their providence or the history of ownership and origin. Understanding how provenence impacts value can be essential for collectors and enthusiasts alike. Providence provides a detailed record of who owned a book, when they owned it, and how it was acquired. This history can significantly increase a book's worth. For example, if a book was owned by a famous historical figure, its value can skyrocket. Imagine a book that belonged to George Washington or Napoleon Bonapart. Such connections make these items highly sought after. Another factor is the association with authors or notable figures. A copy of Mark Twain's Adventures of Huckleberry Finn owned by Jack London would be much more valuable than a standard copy. The story behind the book adds layers of significance that collectors appreciate. The place of origin also plays a role. Books printed in historically important locations or during significant literary periods can command higher prices. For instance, a first edition printed in London during the Victorian era may be more valuable than a similar book printed elsewhere. Historical context is another important aspect. Books linked to major events like World War II or colonial history can have their value influenced by these associations. This can enhance or diminish their worth based on ethical considerations surrounding their acquisition. Market demand is a driving force in the value of rare books. Collectors often seek specific categories such as first editions with author inscriptions. The more desirable the provenence, the higher the demand and value. Physical condition is also critical. A book's original binding, paper quality, and presence of dust jackets all affect its worth. However, some collectors prefer books that show signs of historical use, such as annotations by notable figures. For collectors, verifying provenence is essential. This can be done through documentation or physical evidence within the book, like signatures or book plates. Ensuring the provenence is ethically sound is equally important. Researching the acquisition history helps collectors align with ethical standards, especially for items from contentious periods. Understanding how provenence affects market demand, can guide collectors in making informed decisions. A well doumented provenence can significantly increase a book's value, making it a smart investment for collectors. In the world of rare books, provenence is not just a detail. It is a key factor that intertwines historical significance, ownership, history, and market demand. Collectors who pay attention to these elements can navigate the fascinating realm of rare books with confidence.

- What Is The Emotional Toll Of Trial Data Collection? - Trials and Treatments Uncovered: Kind: captions Language: en [Music] What is the emotional toll of trial data collection? collection? This is an important question for anyone considering participation in clinical trials. The journey through a trial is not just about physical health. It also involves emotional experiences that can be quite intense. First, let's talk about how data collection can increase awareness of health status. Patients often complete questionnaires and report symptoms regularly. This can lead to heightened anxiety or sadness, especially if they notice worsening conditions. Being asked about feelings and health can reminded patients of their illness which can be emotionally challenging. Next, the procedures involved in data collection can also contribute to emotional stress. Frequent visits to the clinic, invasive tests, and complex reporting can feel overwhelming. This burden may lead to frustration or fatigue, making it harder for patients to stay motivated. Moreover, how trial teams manage data collection plays a significant role in emotional well-being. When trial staff listen to patients and address their concerns, it can help ease emotional distress. On the other hand, if patients feel ignored, they may experience feelings of isolation or dissatisfaction. Key moments during the trial can also stir strong emotions. For instance, receiving treatment allocation results can bring feelings of disappointment or confusion. Clear communication from the trial team is essential during these times to provide reassurance and support. It is important for patients to recognize that data collection is more than just physical assessments. It involves emotional engagement as well. Patients should look for trials that include tools to monitor emotional health, like apps or surveys that ask about feelings. This can help ensure timely support when needed. Overall, trial data collection can evoke a range of emotions from fear and sadness to anticipation and trust. Understanding these emotional effects is vital for patient comfort and the overall success of the trial. Patients should inquire about how emotional support is integrated into the data collection process. This awareness can help manage their experiences throughout the trial journey.

The claim that's cited too often that only 9% of plastic was ever recycled might be the most bad-faith argument ever against recycling. Let me quickly break down why this figure, while important, MUST LEAVE THE DEBATE STAND NOW:
1. Guys, gals, both or none of the above, it's not just plastic that's being collected for recycling.
2. In comparison to metals, paper, glass, cooking oil or fabrics, plastics recycling(high-quality that is) is modern unlike these others for which metals might even date back to antiquity.
3. Most plastic ever produced is by recycling's main definition non-recyclable, it is too dangerous, unsafe or a thermoset which aren't recyclable. Elastomers and plastic fibers usually aren't recyclable either.
4. Petroleum companies did massive cover-ups of the environmental impact of plastics, this caused major dumping in nature even of recyclable material.
5. User error plays a big impact, most plastics aren't recycled because they're too contaminated to reprocess and most recyclable plastics aren't captured because of flawed guidance.
Not to mention that this undermines the great efforts of areas which have recycling mandates, low contamination, EPR and don't  export their waste(like BC or Germany)
And it takes into account areas which have no recycling outlets but still produce plastic waste.
Whoever first came up with using that statistic must have been in a very "Context be damned!" mood
It’s like saying “only 5% of cars ever made are still on the road” and using that to argue that driving is pointless—technically true, but wildly misleading if you ignore lifespan, maintenance, and context

  1. @larposan: Counter point to the "blockchain wouldn't work here" argument: The winery would already be part of the verified supply chain since they are also producing new wines. Hence, they should be able to re-vouch for the authenticity of the fixed bottle. Also one could trace back to the winery ,and the exact worker, if they were fraudulently vouching for fake bottles.
  2. @michael2275: Blockchain is only useful for censorship prone activity. Hence bitcoin as digital gold and eventually fully scaled payment network with privacy features is the killer use case. As you said smart contracts are too rigid and still require real world enforcement outside of native tokens  such as bitcoin. Bitcoin has multi trillion dollar potential but all these corporate blockchain solutions and most other cryptocurrencies and dApp/ smart contract platforms are a total joke.

How do we get hard problems? - Mm-hmm. - How do we actually build mathematically hard problems so that we can then use them to build efficient zero-knowledge programs. - [Shang-Hua] Mm-hmm. - And efficient zero-knowledge proofs, right? - I guess also, in quantum computing, you need even harder problems. - Indeed. Absolutely. You know, now that we have the specter of quantum computing - [Shang-Hua] Yes, yes. - coming at us and we all know that quantum computers can break a lot of cryptographic systems. - Yes. A profound challenge. - It's a profound challenge. So can we find new sources of hardness that are quantum-resistant? - [Shang-Hua] That's right. - That even quantum computers can't break. And that's something I've been working on for the last several years. But I'd be very excited to see what happens in that space. - But I'm sure they will motivate beautiful mathematics. - Yes, that's right. You know, one of the great things about the real world is that people in the real world have demands. - That's right. - And those demands often sound impossible. And that's where we come in. It's our job to make the impossible possible.
